reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    SE(3)-invariant linear layer.  Maps node features that are augmented
    with 3-D coordinate information through a linear transform that
    respects rotational and translational invariance.  The layer operates
    on (scalar_features, vector_features) pairs where scalar features
    transform trivially and vector features transform as 3-D vectors.
    Used in equivariant GNNs (EGNN, PaiNN, TFN).
    """

    def __init__(self, scalar_in, scalar_out, vector_in, vector_out):
        super().__init__()
        self.scalar_linear = nn.Linear(scalar_in + vector_in, scalar_out)
        self.vector_weight = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(vector_out, vector_in))
        nn.init.xavier_uniform_(self.vector_weight)
        self.vector_gate = nn.Linear(scalar_in + vector_in, vector_out)

    def forward(
        self, scalar_feat: torch.Tensor, vector_feat: torch.Tensor
    ) -> tuple:
        """
        Args:
            scalar_feat: (B, N, scalar_in) – invariant node features
            vector_feat: (B, N, vector_in, 3) – equivariant vector features
        Returns:
            (scalar_out, vector_out):
                scalar_out: (B, N, scalar_out)
                vector_out: (B, N, vector_out, 3)
        """
        # Invariant scalars from vector norms
        vec_norm = vector_feat.norm(dim=-1)  # (B, N, vector_in)
        combined = torch.cat([scalar_feat, vec_norm], dim=-1)

        s_out = self.scalar_linear(combined)

        # Equivariant vector transform: linear combination of input vectors
        v_out = torch.einsum("oi,bnid->bnod", self.vector_weight, vector_feat)
        # Gating by scalar-derived signal
        gate = torch.sigmoid(self.vector_gate(combined)).unsqueeze(-1)  # (B, N, vector_out, 1)
        v_out = gate * v_out

        return s_out, v_out


scalar_in = 64
scalar_out = 64
vector_in = 16
vector_out = 16
num_nodes = 128
batch_size = 8


def get_inputs():
    s = torch.randn(batch_size, num_nodes, scalar_in)
    v = torch.randn(batch_size, num_nodes, vector_in, 3)
    return [s, v]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [scalar_in, scalar_out, vector_in, vector_out]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that computes the forward pass of the SE(3)-invariant linear layer faster than PyTorch. The layer: - Input: scalar_feat (B, N, scalar_in), vector_feat (B, N, vector_in, 3) - Compute vec_norm = vector_feat.norm(dim=-1) -> shape (B, N, vector_in) - combined = cat([scalar_feat, vec_norm], dim=-1) -> shape (B, N, scalar_in + vector_in) - s_out = combined @ W_s^T + b_s where W_s,b_s from scalar_linear (nn.Linear) - v_out = einsum("oi,bnid->bnod", vector_weight, vector_feat) -> shape (B, N, vector_out, 3). Actually vector_weight shape (vector_out, vector_in). So v_out[b,n,o,i3?] = sum_i vector_weight[o,i] * vector_feat[b,n,i,3]. So it's just a linear combination of input channels into output channels per node, per coordinate. - gate = sigmoid(combined @ W_g^T + b_g) where W_g,b_g from vector_gate (nn.Linear) -> shape (B, N, vector_out). Then unsqueeze(-1) -> (B, N, vector_out, 1), multiply v_out. - Return s_out, v_out. Optimization opportunities: - Fusing all operations into one kernel: compute vec_norms, combine, compute scalar linear and gate linear, compute vector linear, apply sigmoid and multiply. - We can also pre-load weights to shared memory or registers. - For linear layers, we can use standard GEMM but fused with norms and gates. However, writing a fully optimized GEMM is huge. But the sizes are relatively small: scalar_in=64, scalar_out=64, vector_in=16, vector_out=16. Combined size = 80. So scalar_linear is 80->64, vector_gate is 80->16. vector_weight is 16x16. The batch size is B=8, N=128, total nodes = 1024. This is quite small. PyTorch reference will probably launch multiple kernels: norm, cat, linear (GEMM), einsum (maybe bmm or permute+matmul), linear for gate, sigmoid, mul. The overhead of multiple kernels might be significant. A fused CUDA kernel per node (or per thread) should be efficient. Since total nodes = 1024, each node does: - Load vector_feat of shape (vector_in, 3) = 16*3=48 floats. - Compute squared norms per vector_in channel: sum over 3 dims. 16 norms. - scalar_in = 64. So combined is 80 floats. - Compute s_out = W_s * combined + b_s. W_s is (64,80). This is a small matrix-vector multiply per node. 64*80=5120 multiplications per node. - Compute gate = W_g * combined + b_g. W_g is (16,80). 1280 multiplications per node. - Compute v_out = W_v * vector_feat. W_v is (16,16). vector_feat is (16,3). So v_out (16,3) = W_v (16,16) * vector_feat (16,3). This is 16*16*3 = 768 multiplications per node. - Apply sigmoid to gate (16), multiply each of 16 output channels by gate value across 3 coordinates: 16*3 = 48 multiplications. - Write s_out (64), v_out (16*3=48). Total FLOPs per node: ~5120+1280+768+something ~7200 FLOPs. Total nodes: 1024. Total FLOPs: ~7.4 MFLOPs. Very tiny. Memory traffic: read vector_feat (48 floats), read scalar_feat (64 floats), write s_out (64), write v_out (48). Plus weights (read once, maybe cached). Total per node: ~224 floats = ~900 bytes. Total memory: ~900 KB. This is tiny, bandwidth bound if not fused, but compute is also tiny. Fusing into one kernel reduces kernel launch overhead and memory roundtrips for intermediate tensors (combined, vec_norm, gate, v_out before gating). Since the problem size is small, kernel launch overhead is likely dominant. A single kernel should be much faster. Implementation plan: Write a CUDA kernel where each thread handles one node (b,n). So grid of B*N threads, maybe grouped into blocks of e.g. 128 or 256 threads. Within each thread: - Load scalar_feat[scalar_in] into registers. - Load vector_feat[vector_in][3] into registers or shared memory. Since vector_in=16, 3 dims, 48 floats. That's a lot for registers but okay per thread (48 registers). Actually each thread can hold 48 floats, plus weights, etc. - Compute vec_norm_sq or just vec_norm (sqrt). The reference uses .norm(dim=-1) which computes sqrt(sum(x^2)). We need sqrt. But wait: The reference uses `vec_norm = vector_feat.norm(dim=-1)` which is L2 norm with sqrt. Then `combined = torch.cat([scalar_feat, vec_norm], dim=-1)`. So we need sqrt. - Assemble combined vector of length scalar_in + vector_in = 80. We can keep scalar_feat values in registers, then append vec_norm values. So we don't need to allocate a shared array; we can just use the scalar_feat values and then norms when doing the matmuls. - For scalar_linear: W_s is shape (scalar_out, scalar_in+vector_in). We'll store W_s in a 1D array of size scalar_out * combined_size. Same for b_s. - For vector_gate: W_g is shape (vector_out, combined_size). - For vector_weight: W_v is shape (vector_out, vector_in). Since these sizes are small and fixed at compile time (or known at runtime), we can pass them as template parameters for efficiency, but it's okay to pass as int arguments. However, the kernel should handle arbitrary sizes as per constructor args, but the benchmark uses the fixed sizes. We can either write a generic kernel or use template instantiation. Since the kernel is heavily memory-bound but fuses loads, it should be fine. But there's a nuance: if we do one thread per node, each thread reads scalar_feat (64 floats) and vector_feat (48 floats). With 256 threads per block, each block handles 256 nodes. The memory reads for scalar_feat and vector_feat will be coalesced if adjacent threads access adjacent nodes. Since scalar_feat is (B,N,scalar_in), indexing `scalar_feat[b,n,:]`, adjacent n are contiguous in memory if we index by linear index over (B,N). Let's map `idx = b * N + n`. Then for a given channel c, `scalar_feat[b,n,c]` is at offset `(b*N + n)*scalar_in + c`. So adjacent threads (adjacent idx) access the same channel c but offset by scalar_in. This is strided by scalar_in=64 floats = 256 bytes. For coalesced access, adjacent threads should access adjacent memory. Here, thread idx and idx+1 access addresses separated by scalar_in floats. That's contiguous if scalar_in is small? Actually, memory is arranged as: for node 0, channels 0..63, then node 1, channels 0..63, etc. So `scalar_feat[idx, c]` has address `base + (idx * scalar_in + c) * sizeof(float)`. Adjacent threads (idx, idx+1) accessing the same c have addresses separated by `scalar_in * sizeof(float)` = 256 bytes. That's a stride. For 128-bit loads (4 floats), a warp of 32 threads would load 32*256 = 8192 bytes apart, which is not contiguous. The coalescing unit on A100 is 128 bytes per warp? Actually, each warp issues 128-byte transactions. If threads access addresses spaced 256 bytes apart, each thread touches a different 128-byte segment, resulting in 32 transactions of 128 bytes = 4096 bytes loaded, but only 128 bytes actually used (4 bytes per thread). That's terrible bandwidth utilization. To improve memory coalescing, we should have adjacent threads load adjacent floats. That means each thread should handle a specific channel, and we use multiple threads per node. So one node is processed by a block of threads (or warp) that cooperatively load data. Yes, this is important. For a memory-bound tiny kernel, coalescing matters. Let's design a block-per-node approach. Since there are 1024 nodes, we need 1024 blocks. Block size can be e.g. 64 or 128 threads. 1024 blocks is enough to fill the GPU (A100 has 108 SMs, each can run multiple blocks). Each block of 64 threads processes one node. 1024 blocks * 64 threads = 65536 threads. That's a good amount. Better: use 64 threads. - Thread t (0..63) computes scalar_out[t] = dot(W_s[t, :], combined) + b_s[t]. (Assume scalar_out=64). This covers all scalar outputs. - For gate (16 outputs), threads 0..15 compute gate[t] = dot(W_g[t, :], combined) + b_g[t]. - For vector transform (vector_out=16, 3 dims = 48 outputs), threads 0..47 compute one coordinate each: v_out[t/3][t%3] = sum_i W_v[t/3][i] * vector_feat[i][t%3]. Or we can assign 64 threads: threads 0..47 compute one of 48, threads 48..63 are idle for this step. - Then gating: each gate value multiplies 3 coordinates. Threads 0..47 can do that. So within a block, we need to: 1. Load scalar_feat (64 floats) and vector_feat (48 floats) from global memory into shared memory or have each thread load its needed elements. - For scalar_feat: we need all 64 values for combined. We can have thread 0..63 each load one scalar_feat float. Let's store in shared memory `s_scalar[64]`. - For vector_feat: we need all 48 values. We can have thread 0..47 each load one coordinate. But vector_feat shape is (Vin,3). Let's store in `s_vector[48]` (or `s_vector[Vin][3]`). 2. And maybe also `s_norm[t]`. - Threads 0..63 also copy scalar_feat into s_combined[0..scalar_in-1] (each thread copies its own). - __syncthreads(). 3. Compute scalar_linear and vector_gate: - Thread t (0..63) computes scalar_out[t] = dot(W_s[t*combined_size : (t+1)*combined_size], s_combined) + b_s[t]. Since combined_size = 80. That's a dot product of length 80. We can unroll or loop. With 64 threads, each doing a loop of 80, it's 5120 iterations total. Fine. - Threads 0..15 also compute gate[t] = dot(W_g[t*combined_size : (t+1)*combined_size], s_combined) + b_g[t]. After computing, apply sigmoid. - __syncthreads() maybe not needed if we store scalar_out in shared and gate in shared. 4. Compute vector linear: - Thread t (0..47) computes v_out[t] = sum_{i=0}^{Vin-1} W_v[(t/3)*Vin + i] * s_vector[i*3 + (t%3)]. - That's a loop of Vin=16 per thread. 48*16=768 iterations. - __syncthreads(). 5. Apply gate: - Thread t (0..47) reads gate[t/3] and multiplies v_out[t]. - Write v_out to global memory. - Thread t (0..63) writes scalar_out[t]. This is quite clean. Memory coalescing for loads of scalar_feat and vector_feat: - Since each block handles one node, reading scalar_feat for that node is not coalesced across threads within the block because all threads are reading from the same node? Wait, if each block handles one node, then threads in the block read different channels of the same node. In memory, channels are contiguous. So thread 0 reads channel 0, thread 1 reads channel 1, etc. So adjacent threads read adjacent floats. This is perfectly coalesced! Excellent. - Similarly for vector_feat: thread 0 reads first float of vector_feat for that node, thread 1 reads next float. That's not coalesced. However, weights are read-only and can be cached in L1/L2. Actually all threads read different rows, so shared memory doesn't help with broadcast. But if we transpose W_s so that columns are contiguous for coalescing? Let's think: if W_s is column-major (combined_size, scalar_out), then thread t reads column t, and adjacent threads read adjacent columns. But each dot product requires reading the entire column? No, dot product reads row t. If stored column-major, reading row t is strided by scalar_out. That's also strided. We can keep row-major and accept the strided reads, since it's cached. Alternatively, we can compute the dot product by having threads within a warp collaborate: each thread reads a chunk of combined and a chunk of W_s, accumulate partial sums, then warp-reduce. That's fine (shared memory). The access to W_v is row o, contiguous across i (since W_v is row-major (Vout, Vin)). So for a given thread, W_v reads are contiguous. Good. Actually if stored as `float W_v[Vout][Vin]`, threads reading the same row access the same banks sequentially or broadcast (depending on compute capability). On A100, broadcast is supported. So fine. But we don't need to put W_v in shared memory. Let's design the kernel signature: ```cpp torch::Tensor se3layer_forward( torch::Tensor scalar_feat, // (B, N, scalar_in) torch::Tensor vector_feat, // (B, N, vector_in, 3) torch::Tensor scalar_weight, // (scalar_out, scalar_in+vector_in) torch::Tensor scalar_bias, // (scalar_out) torch::Tensor vector_weight, // (vector_out, vector_in) torch::Tensor gate_weight, // (vector_out, scalar_in+vector_in) torch::Tensor gate_bias // (vector_out) ) -> std::vector<torch::Tensor> { s_out, v_out } ``` But we need to handle `scalar_in`, `scalar_out`, `vector_in`, `vector_out` dynamically. In CUDA kernel: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void se3kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_feat, const scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_feat, const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_bias, const scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate_bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_out, scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_out, int B, int N, int scalar_in, int scalar_out, int vector_in, int vector_out) { // linear node index int node_idx = blockIdx.x; // one block per node int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // e.g., 64 if (node_idx >= B * N) return; int b = node_idx / N; int n = node_idx % N; int combined_size = scalar_in + vector_in; // Shared memory extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* s_scalar = (scalar_t*)smem; // size scalar_in scalar_t* s_vector = s_scalar + scalar_in; // size vector_in * 3 scalar_t* s_combined = s_vector + vector_in * 3; // size combined_size scalar_t* s_scalar_out = s_combined + combined_size; // size scalar_out scalar_t* s_gate = s_scalar_out + scalar_out; // size vector_out scalar_t* s_vector_out = s_gate + vector_out; // size vector_out * 3 // Load scalar_feat for (int i = tid; i < scalar_in; i += num_threads) { s_scalar[i] = scalar_feat[((b * N + n) * scalar_in) + i]; } // Load vector_feat int vector_feat_size = vector_in * 3; for (int i = tid; i < vector_feat_size; i += num_threads) { s_vector[i] = vector_feat[((b * N + n) * vector_feat_size) + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Build combined: scalar part + vector norms // Copy scalar part for (int i = tid; i < scalar_in; i += num_threads) { s_combined[i] = s_scalar[i]; } // Compute vector norms // We'll have each thread handle some vector_in channels for (int i = tid; i < vector_in; i += num_threads) { scalar_t x = s_vector[i * 3 + 0]; scalar_t y = s_vector[i * 3 + 1]; scalar_t z = s_vector[i * 3 + 2]; s_combined[scalar_in + i] = sqrtf(x*x + y*y + z*z); } __syncthreads(); // Scalar linear for (int o = tid; o < scalar_out; o += num_threads) { scalar_t accum = 0; const scalar_t* w_row = scalar_weight + o * combined_size; for (int i = 0; i < combined_size; ++i) { accum += w_row[i] * s_combined[i]; } s_scalar_out[o] = accum + scalar_bias[o]; } // Vector gate for (int o = tid; o < vector_out; o += num_threads) { scalar_t accum = 0; const scalar_t* w_row = gate_weight + o * combined_size; for (int i = 0; i < combined_size; ++i) { accum += w_row[i] * s_combined[i]; } scalar_t g = accum + gate_bias[o]; // sigmoid g = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-g)); s_gate[o] = g; } __syncthreads(); // Vector linear int v_out_size = vector_out * 3; for (int idx = tid; idx < v_out_size; idx += num_threads) { int o = idx / 3; int d = idx % 3; scalar_t accum = 0; const scalar_t* w_row = vector_weight + o * vector_in; for (int i = 0; i < vector_in; ++i) { accum += w_row[i] * s_vector[i * 3 + d]; } s_vector_out[idx] = accum; } __syncthreads(); // Apply gate for (int idx = tid; idx < v_out_size; idx += num_threads) { int o = idx / 3; s_vector_out[idx] = s_vector_out[idx] * s_gate[o]; } __syncthreads(); // Write outputs for (int i = tid; i < scalar_out; i += num_threads) { scalar_out[((b * N + n) * scalar_out) + i] = s_scalar_out[i]; } for (int i = tid; i < v_out_size; i += num_threads) { vector_out[((b * N + n) * v_out_size) + i] = s_vector_out[i]; } } ``` This should work. We need to compile the extension at module load time using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. The C++ code shouldn't include a pybind module. Let's write the inline C++ code. C++ source: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void se3_forward_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_feat, const scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_feat, const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_bias, const scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate_bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_out, scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_out, int B, int N, int scalar_in, int scalar_out, int vector_in, int vector_out) { int node_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nt = blockDim.x; if (node_idx >= B * N) return; int combined_size = scalar_in + vector_in; int vfeat_size = vector_in * 3; int vout_size = vector_out * 3; extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* s_scalar = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(smem); scalar_t* s_vector = s_scalar + scalar_in; scalar_t* s_norm = s_vector + vfeat_size; scalar_t* s_gate = s_norm + vector_in; // load scalar_feat for (int i = tid; i < scalar_in; i += nt) { s_scalar[i] = scalar_feat[node_idx * scalar_in + i]; } // load vector_feat for (int i = tid; i < vfeat_size; i += nt) { s_vector[i] = vector_feat[node_idx * vfeat_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); // compute norms for (int i = tid; i < vector_in; i += nt) { scalar_t x = s_vector[i * 3]; scalar_t y = s_vector[i * 3 + 1]; scalar_t z = s_vector[i * 3 + 2]; s_norm[i] = sqrtf(x*x + y*y + z*z); } __syncthreads(); // scalar linear const scalar_t* w_s = scalar_weight; for (int o = tid; o < scalar_out; o += nt) { scalar_t acc = scalar_bias[o]; const scalar_t* row = w_s + o * combined_size; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < scalar_in; ++i) { acc += row[i] * s_scalar[i]; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < vector_in; ++i) { acc += row[scalar_in + i] * s_norm[i]; } scalar_out[node_idx * scalar_out + o] = acc; } // vector gate const scalar_t* w_g = gate_weight; for (int o = tid; o < vector_out; o += nt) { scalar_t acc = gate_bias[o]; const scalar_t* row = w_g + o * combined_size; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < scalar_in; ++i) { acc += row[i] * s_scalar[i]; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < vector_in; ++i) { acc += row[scalar_in + i] * s_norm[i]; } scalar_t g = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); s_gate[o] = g; } __syncthreads(); // vector linear + gate const scalar_t* w_v = vector_weight; for (int idx = tid; idx < vout_size; idx += nt) { int o = idx / 3; int d = idx % 3; scalar_t acc = 0.0f; const scalar_t* row = w_v + o * vector_in; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < vector_in; ++i) { acc += row[i] * s_vector[i * 3 + d]; } acc *= s_gate[o]; vector_out[node_idx * vout_size + idx] = acc; } } ``` Wait, `#pragma unroll` with variable loop bounds is ignored by the compiler, but it doesn't cause errors. We can leave it. Actually, we can't easily unroll without template parameters. But maybe we can add a specialized path. The expression `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc))` works for `float` but if `scalar_t` is `double`, `expf` takes float. We should use `exp` or `expf` conditionally. But on CUDA, `exp` for `float` is not defined? Actually `exp(float)` is available if we include `<cmath>` or use `::exp`. But in device code, `expf` is the standard CUDA intrinsic for float, and `exp` for double. To be safe, we can write: ```cpp scalar_t g = scalar_t(1) / (scalar_t(1) + static_cast<scalar_t>(1) / (static_cast<scalar_t>(1) + exp(-acc))); ``` Actually we can define an inline helper: ```cpp template <typename T> __device__ __forceinline__ T sigmoid(T x) { return T(1) / (T(1) + exp(-x)); } ``` But `exp` for float in device code might resolve to `::exp(float)` which is supported. Let's draft the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> template <typename T> __device__ __forceinline__ T sigmoid_device(T x) { return T(1) / (T(1) + exp(-x)); } template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void se3_forward_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_feat, const scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_feat, const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_bias, const scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate_weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate_bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ scalar_out, scalar_t* __restrict__ vector_out, int B, int N, int scalar_in, int scalar_out, int vector_in, int vector_out) { int node_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nt = blockDim.x; if (node_idx >= B * N) return; int combined_size = scalar_in + vector_in; int vfeat_size = vector_in * 3; int vout_size = vector_out * 3; extern __shared__ char smem[]; i < scalar_in; i += nt) { s_scalar[i] = scalar_feat[node_idx * scalar_in + i]; } // Load vector features for (int i = tid; i < vfeat_size; i += nt) { s_vector[i] = vector_feat[node_idx * vfeat_size + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute vector norms for (int i = tid; i < vector_in; i += nt) { scalar_t x = s_vector[i * 3]; scalar_t y = s_vector[i * 3 + 1]; scalar_t z = s_vector[i * 3 + 2]; s_norm[i] = sqrtf(x*x + y*y + z*z); vector_out[node_idx * vout_size + idx] = acc; } } torch::Tensor se3_forward( torch::Tensor scalar_feat, torch::Tensor vector_feat, torch::Tensor scalar_weight, torch::Tensor scalar_bias, torch::Tensor vector_weight, torch::Tensor gate_weight, torch::Tensor gate_bias) { int B = scalar_feat.size(0); auto vector_out_t = torch::empty({B, N, vector_out, 3}, vector_feat.options()); int nodes = B * N; int threads = 64; int vfeat_size = vector_in * 3; int shared_mem_size = (scalar_in + vfeat_size + vector_in + vector_out) * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(scalar_feat.scalar_type(), "se3_forward", [&] { se3_forward_kernel<scalar_t><<<nodes, threads, shared_mem_size>>>( scalar_feat.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), vector_feat.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), scalar_weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), scalar_bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), vector_weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), gate_weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), gate_bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), scalar_out_t.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), vector_out_t.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), B, N, scalar_in, scalar_out, vector_in, vector_out); }); return {scalar_out_t, vector_out_t}; } """ ``` Actually, the C++ wrapper returns `std::vector<torch::Tensor>`, but we need to match the pybind return type. Let's think: scalar_in=64, scalar_out=64, vector_in=16, vector_out=16, vout_size=48. With 64 threads: - scalar loads: 64 threads, each loads 1 -> 1 transaction per warp? Actually 32 threads load 32 adjacent floats = 128 bytes, perfect coalescing. 2 warps, 2 transactions. - vector loads: 48 floats. First warp loads 32 floats (128 bytes), second warp loads 16 floats (64 bytes). Coalesced. - norms: 16 threads active. First 16 threads of warp 0 active. Loads from shared memory, no coalescing issue. - scalar linear: 64 threads active. Each thread reads weight row (80 floats). Weight reads are not coalesced across threads, but cached. - gate: 16 threads active. - vector linear: 48 threads active (threads 0..47). Each reads weight row (16 floats). With 32 threads: - scalar loads: 32 threads, each loads 2 -> coalesced. - vector loads: 32 threads, each loads 1 or 2 -> coalesced. - norms: 16 threads active in warp 0. - scalar linear: 32 threads, each does 2 outputs. - gate: 16 threads active. - vector linear: 32 threads, each does 1 or 2 outputs. 32 threads might be better because all active threads are in one warp, reducing warp count but fully utilizing that warp. However, with 64 threads, some operations have idle threads in warp 1. The warp scheduler still schedules warp 1 even if some threads are predicated off. But instructions are still issued. It wastes some instruction issue bandwidth but not a huge deal. However, block size 32 gives 1 warp per block. 1024 blocks => 1024 warps. Block size 64 gives 2 warps per block => 2048 warps. More warps generally help hide latency. But since the kernel is very short, latency hiding might not matter. I think block size 64 is a good start. But we can also try block size 128: for scalar loads, 64 threads active, 64 idle. For vector loads, 48 active, 80 idle. For scalar linear, 64 active, 64 idle. For vector linear, 48 active, 80 idle. So effective warps are same as 64 threads? Not exactly: a warp is either partially active or fully active. Warp 0 (threads 0..31) might have all active for scalar linear? Actually scalar linear loops over o = tid; o < scalar_out; o += nt. With nt=128 and scalar_out=64, only tid 0..63 execute. So warp 0 (0..31) and warp 1 (32..63) are fully active. Warps 2 and 3 (64..127) are fully inactive. So the scheduler will quickly skip warps 2 and 3. So effective instruction issue is same as block size 64. But total warps = 4096 vs 2048. The scheduler may waste time scanning inactive warps. So block size 64 is better. Thus, I will stick with 64 threads. One thing: `threads = 64` is hardcoded in the host wrapper. The output of a linear layer with ~80 inputs has variance ~1, so pre-activation is ~N(0,1). So `exp(-x)` won't overflow/underflow for typical values. So naive sigmoid is fine. 3. For 80 terms, the difference should be small, well within 1e-4. Let's verify: typical dot product of 80 terms, each ~1, sum ~80. reasoning
Let me analyze the reference model: 1. `vector_feat` has shape `(B, N, vector_in, 3)` 2. `vec_norm = vector_feat.norm(dim=-1)` gives `(B, N, vector_in)` 3. `combined = torch.cat([scalar_feat, vec_norm], dim=-1)` gives `(B, N, scalar_in + vector_in)` 4. `s_out = self.scalar_linear(combined)` - linear layer 5. `v_out = torch.einsum("oi,bnid->bnod", self.vector_weight, vector_feat)` - this is a batched matrix multiply 6. `gate = torch.sigmoid(self.vector_gate(combined)).unsqueeze(-1)` 7. `v_out = gate * v_out` Let me compute the dimensions: - B = 8, N = 128 - scalar_in = 64, scalar_out = 64 - vector_in = 16, vector_out = 16 For the scalar linear: - Input: 64 + 16 = 80, Output: 64 - Weights: 80 x 64, bias: 64 For the vector gate: - Input: 80, Output: 16 - Weights: 80 x 16, bias: 16 For vector_weight: - 16 x 16 The einsum "oi,bnid->bnod" means for each b, n, multiply vector_weight (16x16) with vector_feat (16x3) to get (16x3). This is essentially a matrix multiplication per (b,n). The key operations are: 1. Compute L2 norm of vectors: sqrt(sum(x^2)) 2. Concatenate scalars with norms 3. Linear layer for s_out 4. Linear layer for gate 5. Matrix multiply vector_weight with vector_feat 6. Sigmoid activation 7. Multiply gate with v_out The main optimization opportunities: 1. Fuse the norm computation with concatenation and the linear layers 2. Fuse the vector transformation with gating 3. Use custom CUDA kernel to avoid intermediate allocations 4. Take advantage of the small sizes to do everything in registers/shared memory Let me think about kernel design. The sizes are: - B*N = 8*128 = 1024 independent operations - For scalar path: each "row" processes 80 inputs to 64+16=80 outputs - For vector path: each "row" does 16x16 MM with 16x3 Since each (b,n) is independent, we can parallelize over B*N. For each (b,n): 1. Compute norms of 16 vectors of dimension 3 2. Concatenate with 64 scalars 3. Compute scalar linear (80 -> 64) 4. Compute gate linear (80 -> 16) + sigmoid 5. Compute v_out = W_v @ v_feat (16x16 @ 16x3 = 16x3) 6. Scale v_out by gate This can all be done in one kernel! We load state, compute, and store results. Let me think about thread assignment: - We have B*N = 1024 items - Each item needs to compute: - Norms (16 * 3 = 48 floats) - Scalar linear: effectively a mat-vec of 80x64 - Gate linear: mat-vec of 80x16 - Vector transform: 16x16 @ 16x3 - Elementwise sigmoid and multiply We could assign one warp (32 threads) per (b,n) item, or multiple items per block. Let me think more carefully: - A block of 128 threads could handle 4 (b,n) items with 32 threads each - Or a block of 256 threads could handle 8 items For the linear layers with small dimensions: - scalar_linear: 80x64 matrix - vector_gate: 80x16 matrix - vector_weight: 16x16 matrix Loading weights from shared memory would be ideal. We can load all weights into shared memory once per block. Weights sizes: - scalar_weight: 80*64 = 5120 floats ≈ 20KB - scalar_bias: 64 floats - gate_weight: 80*16 = 1280 floats ≈ 5KB - gate_bias: 16 floats - vector_weight: 16*16 = 256 floats ≈ 1KB - Total weights: ~26KB - fits in shared memory easily! But B*N = 1024, and weights are shared across all. If we load weights in shared mem, we need to coordinate. Actually, let me think about this differently. Using shared memory for weights is great if multiple threads in a block use them. Option 1: Each block has multiple (b,n) items processed by warps, weights in shared memory. Option 2: Each thread handles one (b,n) item, registers for everything. Option 2 might work well because: - The input per item is 64 + 16*3 = 112 floats - The computation is: 2 linear layers + 1 matmul + elementwise + norms - For H100, we have plenty of registers Actually, let me think about registers. Per thread for one (b,n): - Input scalars: 64 floats → 256 bytes in registers is too much - Actually we don't need all in registers at once. We can stream through. For the scalar_linear output: 64 floats. We could compute these one or a few at a time. Better approach: use 2D tiling or have each warp handle one (b,n). Let me design a kernel where each thread block handles multiple (b,n), and within each block, threads cooperate. For scalar_linear with out_features=64 and in_features=80: - We can have 64 threads each compute one output element using a dot product of the input with one column of weight. Actually, more standard approach: - For each (b,n), we need to compute: y[j] = sum_i(x[i] * W[i,j]) + b[j] - We can assign T threads per (b,n), where each thread computes a slice of outputs. For B*N = 1024 items, we launch 1024 threads total. Block size of 128, so 8 blocks. Per thread: 1. Load 64 scalar inputs 2. Load 48 vector inputs (16 vectors x 3 dims) 3. Compute 16 norms: norm_i = sqrt(v[i,0]^2 + v[i,1]^2 + v[i,2]^2) 4. At 3.35 TB/s: ~0.28 microseconds. So this is memory-bound. The key is to avoid temporary allocations and minimize memory traffic. With a fused kernel, we read inputs once, compute everything, write outputs once. This avoids all intermediate tensors (norms, combined, gate unsqueezed, etc.). Let me now write out the kernel design more concretely. For each (b,n) assigned to a thread group of 16 threads: Thread `t` in group `g`: - Load scalar_feat[b, n, :] into shared memory or registers (all 16 threads in group cooperate) - Actually, each thread can load some portion - With 16 threads and 64 scalars: each thread loads 4 scalars - Load vector_feat[b, n, :, :] - With 16 threads and 48 floats (16*3): each thread loads 3 floats Then each thread computes its assigned outputs. For scalar_linear outputs assigned to thread t: Output indices: t, t+16, t+32, t+48 (4 outputs) For each output j: sum = bias[j] for i in 0..79: sum += combined[i] * W_scalar[i, j] s_out[b, n, j] = sum For gate output assigned to thread t: Output index: t sum = bias[t] for i in 0..79: sum += combined[i] * W_gate[i, t] gate[t] = 1 / (1 + exp(-sum)) For vector output row t: For j in 0..2: sum = 0 for k in 0..15: sum += W_vector[t, k] * v[k, j] v_out[b, n, t, j] = gate[t] * sum This is quite clean! Let me count memory traffic per thread group (16 threads): Read: - scalar_feat: 64 floats = 256 bytes - vector_feat: 48 floats = 192 bytes - scalar_weight (for 4 outputs each = 64 outputs): but each thread reads cols t, t+16, t+32, t+48, all 80 rows each = 4*80 = 320 floats per thread = 1280 bytes. 16 threads = 20480 bytes total reads from weight. - gate_weight: 1*80 = 80 floats per thread = 320 bytes. 16 threads = 5120 bytes. - vector_weight: 1*16 = 16 floats per thread = 64 bytes. 16 threads = 1024 bytes. Write: - s_out: 64 floats = 256 bytes - v_out: 48 floats = 192 bytes If we assume weights are in L2 (which they should be after first few blocks), the actual HBM traffic is mainly inputs and outputs. Total per-item HBM traffic ≈ 256 + 192 + 256 + 192 = 896 bytes. Wait, but weight reads also count if they miss L2. However, weights are only 26KB total, so once loaded in L2, all subsequent reads are L2 hits. Total execution characteristics: - 1024 items - Each item does ~14.5K FLOPs - Total FLOPs: ~14.8 MFLOPs - Total HBM traffic: ~917KB inputs + outputs + ~26KB weights = ~943KB - Arithmetic intensity: 14.8M / 943K ≈ 15.7 FLOPs/byte At 67 TFLOPS / 3.35 TB/s = 20 FLOPs/byte for compute-boundedness. Since 15.7 < 20, we're memory-bound. The key question is: can we beat PyTorch's multiple-kernel approach by fusing everything? PyTorch reference does: 1. vector_feat.norm(dim=-1): reads 48 floats, writes 16 floats, computes norms 2. torch.cat: reads 64 + 16 floats, writes 80 floats 3. scalar_linear: reads 80 floats, weights, writes 64 floats 4. einsum: reads 48 floats, weights, writes 48 floats 5. vector_gate: reads 80 floats, weights, writes 16 floats 6. sigmoid: reads 16, writes 16 7. unsqueeze + multiply: reads 48 + 16, writes 48 Total PyTorch HBM traffic: - Inputs read once: 256 + 192 = 448 bytes - Outputs written once: 256 + 192 = 448 bytes - But intermediates written and read multiple times Actually, in PyTorch, each op is a separate kernel launching loads/stores. So: 1. norm: read 192, write 64 (for vec_norm) 2. cat: effectively just reorganization, but PyTorch might fuse or allocate new tensor. Actually, it allocates new tensor. read 256 + 64, write 320 (combined) 3. scalar_linear: read 320, read weights, write 256 4. einsum: read 192, read weights, write 192 5. vector_gate: read 320, read weights, write 64 (before sigmoid) 6. sigmoid: read 64, write 64 7. gate multiply: read 192 + 64, write 192 Total reads: 192 + 320 + 256 + 320 + 64 + 192 + 64 = 1408? No, let me be more careful. With eager mode (no fusion): 1. norm kernel: reads vector_feat (192B), writes vec_norm (64B) 2. cat kernel: reads scalar_feat (256B) + vec_norm (64B), writes combined (320B) 3. scalar_linear: reads combined (320B) + weights (20KB) + bias, writes s_out (256B) 4. einsum: reads vector_feat (192B) + W_v (1KB), writes v_out_unscaled (192B) 5. vector_gate: reads combined (320B) + weights (5KB) + bias, writes gate_pre_sigmoid (64B) 6. sigmoid: reads gate_pre_sigmoid (64B), writes gate (64B) 7. unsqueeze+mul: reads gate (64B) + v_out_unscaled (192B), writes v_out (192B) Total HBM traffic (ignoring weights since they're cached in L2): Reads: 192 + 320 + 320 + 192 + 320 + 64 + 256 = 1664 bytes Writes: 64 + 320 + 256 + 192 + 64 + 64 + 192 = 1152 bytes Total: ~2816 bytes per item With our fused kernel: 896 bytes per item That's a 3.14x reduction in memory traffic! So even with imperfect thread utilization, we should get a significant speedup. Let me now think about implementation. ### CUDA Kernel Design ``` // One block handles multiple (B, N) items // Each warp (or group of 16 threads) handles one item // Grid: (num_blocks) where each block has 128 threads = 8 items // Block size: 128 // For thread assignment within block: // item_id = threadIdx.x / 16 // lane_id = threadIdx.x % 16 ``` Let me write the kernel. Actually, let me also think about vector loads. Since scalar_feat is contiguous in the last dimension (64), we can use float4 loads (4 floats at a time). For scalar_feat of 64 floats: 16 float4 loads for 16 threads. Perfect! For vector_feat of 48 floats: not a multiple of 4... 48/16 = 3 floats per thread. Could do float + float2. For weights: - scalar_weight is (80, 64) in PyTorch linear, which is stored transposed for cuBLAS, but in PyTorch Linear, weight is (out_features, in_features) = (64, 80). So W[j, i] gives weight from input i to output j. - When we access W[i, j] with fixed j and varying i, we have strided access. Better to transpose. Actually, in PyTorch nn.Linear, weight is (out_features, in_features), stored row-major. So W[j, i] is the weight connecting input i to output j. For output j, we need all W[j, 0..79]. If thread t accesses W[t, i] for i = 0..79, and W is stored as (64, 80), then each thread accesses a different row, which is contiguous. Good! Similarly, gate_weight is (16, 80), so W_gate[t, i] is contiguous. vector_weight is (16, 16), so W_vector[t, k] is contiguous. So the weight access patterns are actually quite good for coalescing if adjacent threads access adjacent rows. However, each thread in our 16-thread group accesses the same row? No - thread t accesses row t for gate and vector, and rows t, t+16, t+32, t+48 for scalar. So threads 0..15 access different rows. This is actually great for coalescing if multiple items are being processed. Wait, within a warp (32 threads), we'd have: - For item 0: threads 0-15 access rows 0-15 of W_gate - For item 1: threads 16-31 access rows 0-15 of W_gate So if item 0 and item 1 read the same rows of W_gate, the reads from global memory will be broadcast from L2 cache. Since all blocks read the same weights, this should be well cached. For scalar weights, within a warp: - Threads 0-15 access rows 0,16,32,48, then 1,17,33,49, etc. for item 0 - Threads 16-31 access the same pattern for item 1 Actually, each thread loops over i from 0 to 79 and accesses W_scalar[row, i]. Adjacent threads access different rows. So for fixed i, threads t0, t1 access W_scalar[r0, i], W_scalar[r1, i]. These are separated by 80 floats = 320 bytes. So within a 128-byte cache line, there are weights for multiple i values of the same row, but different rows are not in the same cache line. Since different threads access different rows, each thread gets its own cache lines. There might be cache contention but since weights are only 20KB, they're in L2. Hmm, maybe this isn't optimal for coalescing. But for a kernel that's memory-bound on inputs/outputs, weight memory bandwidth is less critical. Let me think about whether we should transpose weights in Python and pass them to the kernel in a more optimal layout. If we transpose scalar_weight to (80, 64), then for output j, W_T[i, j] is the weight. But thread t computes outputs t, t+16, t+32, t+48. So for fixed i, we need W[i, t], W[i, t+16], W[i, t+32], W[i, t+48]. This is scattered access. Worse. Actually, the original layout W[j, i] where j is output and i is input is already good for us because thread t reads row t continuously. For vector_weight (16, 16), the original layout is also fine because thread t reads row t continuously. For gate_weight (16, 80), thread t reads row t continuously. Fine. So PyTorch's default weight layout is already good for our access pattern! Now let me write the actual CUDA code. First, I need to figure out how to compute lane and item indices correctly. ```cuda __global__ void se3_layer_kernel( const float* scalar_feat, const float* vector_feat, const float* scalar_weight, const float* scalar_bias, const float* vector_weight, const float* gate_weight, const float* gate_bias, float* scalar_out, float* vector_out, int B, int N, int scalar_in, int scalar_out_dim, int vector_in, int vector_out_dim ) { const int total_items = B * N; const int threads_per_item = 16; const int items_per_block = blockDim.x / threads_per_item; int item_idx = blockIdx.x * items_per_block + threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int lane = threadIdx.x % threads_per_item; if (item_idx >= total_items) return; int b = item_idx / N; int n = item_idx % N; // Pointers to this item's data const float* s_in = scalar_feat + item_idx * scalar_in; const float* v_in = vector_feat + item_idx * vector_in * 3; float* s_out_ptr = scalar_out + item_idx * scalar_out_dim; float* v_out_ptr = vector_out + item_idx * vector_out_dim * 3; // Load scalar inputs (64 floats) - each thread loads 4 float s_local[4]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; i++) { s_local[i] = s_in[lane * 4 + i]; } // Load vector inputs and compute norms float v_local[3]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 3; i++) { v_local[i] = v_in[lane * 3 + i]; } float norm = sqrtf(v_local[0]*v_local[0] + v_local[1]*v_local[1] + v_local[2]*v_local[2]); // Now we have in registers: // Thread lane: s_local[0..3] = scalar_in[lane*4 + (0..3)] // v_local[0..2] = vector_in[lane*3 + (0..2)] // norm = norm of the lane-th vector // Compute scalar linear outputs assigned to this thread // Thread lane computes outputs: lane, lane+16, lane+32, lane+48 #pragma unroll for (int out_idx = 0; out_idx < 4; out_idx++) { int j = lane + out_idx * 16; float sum = scalar_bias[j]; // Sum over scalar inputs (64) #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { // Who has scalar input i? Thread i/4, element i%4 float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFF, s_local[i % 4], i / 4); sum += val * scalar_weight[j * 80 + i]; } // Sum over vector norms (16) #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFF, norm, i); sum += val * scalar_weight[j * 80 + 64 + i]; } s_out_ptr[j] = sum; } // Compute gate for this thread float gate_sum = gate_bias[lane]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFF, s_local[i % 4], i / 4); gate_sum += val * gate_weight[lane * 80 + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFF, norm, i); gate_sum += val * gate_weight[lane * 80 + 64 + i]; } float gate_val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate_sum)); // Compute vector output for this thread's row #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 3; j++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 16; k++) { float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFF, v_local[j], k); sum += val * vector_weight[lane * 16 + k]; } v_out_ptr[lane * 3 + j] = gate_val * sum; } } ``` Wait, I used `__shfl_sync` to broadcast values within the 16-thread group. But `__shfl_sync` works within a warp (32 threads), and our 16-thread group might be the lower half of a warp. Since threads 0-15 are in the same warp, `__shfl_sync(0xFFFF, val, src_lane)` works. But we need to make sure the mask covers the right threads. Actually, in CUDA, `__shfl_sync(unsigned mask, T var, int srcLane)` reads from `srcLane` within the warp. The mask must have the calling thread's bit set. If we call with mask=0xFFFF, all threads in the warp participate. In our 16-thread group within a warp of 32, threads 0-15 are one group, threads 16-31 are another. When thread 0 does `__shfl_sync(0xFFFF, val, 5)`, it reads from lane 5 within the warp, which is correct. Instead of shuffle, what if we use shared memory to broadcast the inputs? ### Shared Memory Broadcast Alternative Store inputs in shared memory: - `s_shared[64]` for scalar inputs - `n_shared[16]` for vector norms - `v_shared[16][3]` for vector inputs Each thread reads from shared memory instead of shuffling. Let me try the shared memory approach first because it's cleaner and easier to reason about. If it's not fast enough, we can try shuffle. Actually, I just realized something. If each block has 8 items and we use shared memory for all 8 items' inputs, we need __syncthreads() between loading and computing. But since each item is independent within its 16 threads, and different items don't communicate at all, we could store inputs in shared memory without much overhead. However, there might be bank conflicts. Shared memory has 32 banks on modern GPUs. If threads within a warp access different banks, it's fast. If they access the same bank, it's serialized. For combined_shared[item_local][i]: - If 16 threads in a warp access combined_shared[0][0..15] (assuming item_local=0), each thread accesses a different bank. No conflict. - If they access combined_shared[item_local][same_i], that's a broadcast. No conflict. For weights (in shared memory or global): - Thread t accesses weight[j * 80 + i] where j depends on t. - If j values are separated by multiples of 32 * 4 = 128 bytes, they might hit the same bank. Actually, if weights are in shared memory: - scalar_weight is [64][80] (or actually [64][80] if we think of it as 64 rows of 80 elements each) - Thread t (within a half-warp) accesses rows t, t+16, etc. - Row t starts at t * 80 floats = t * 320 bytes. - Bank = (t * 320 / 4) % 32 = t * 80 % 32. - For t = 0..15: banks = 0, 16, 0, 16, 0, 16... - Hmm, many bank conflicts! Wait, let me recalculate. Each float is 4 bytes, so 32 banks handle 128 bytes. Bank = (address / 4) % 32. Row t starts at address t * 80 * 4 = t * 320 bytes. For t = 0: bank 0 t = 1: 320/4 = 80 % 32 = 16 t = 2: 160 % 32 = 0 t = 3: 240 % 32 = 16 ... So for threads 0,2,4,... they all access bank 0! That's a massive bank conflict for 8 threads. For threads 1,3,5,... they all access bank 16. So if we have 16 threads accessing scalar_weight[j][i] with fixed i and j = lane, lane+16, etc., we get 8-way bank conflicts for bank 0 and 8-way for bank 16. This is bad. However, if weights are in global memory (cached in L1), we don't have shared memory bank conflicts. Bank conflicts only happen for shared memory. So storing weights in shared memory causes bank conflicts! Better to keep weights in global memory (L2 cached) and only use shared memory for the inputs which have better access patterns. For combined_shared[item_local][i]: - All 16 threads in a group access the same item_local but with different i values. - Thread t accesses combined_shared[0][t] for some phase, then [0][t+16], etc. - Address for [0][t]: t * 4 bytes. Bank = t % 32. Threads 0..15 access banks 0..15. No conflicts! - Address for [0][t+16]: (t+16) * 4. Bank = (t+16) % 32. Still no conflicts within 16 threads. So inputs in shared memory have good access patterns. Weights in shared memory have bad patterns. Conclusion: keep weights in global memory (they'll be L2 cached), use shared memory only for inputs/outputs that are shared among threads. Actually, we might not even need shared memory for inputs if we use the shuffle approach. But inputs in shared memory are accessed with no bank conflicts, so that's good. Let me refine the design: ### Refined Design Keep weights in global/L2 memory. Use shared memory for combined inputs and vectors (needed for matrix multiply). This is a broadcast from shared memory, which is optimal! For the weights `W[lane][k]`, each thread accesses a different row (`lane`), but same column `k`. Hmm, actually `W[lane][k]` with `lane` varying across threads means that for fixed k, threads access W[0][k], W[1][k], etc. If W is stored row-major as [16][16], then W[lane][k] is at address lane*64 + k*4 bytes. For k=0, threads access addresses 0, 64, 128, 192, etc. These are 64 bytes apart. A cache line is 128 bytes, so addresses 0, 64 are in different cache lines. Actually, a cache line holds 32 floats = 128 bytes. So W[0][0..31] is one cache line. W[1][0..31] is another cache line. So each thread accesses a different cache line. With 16 threads accessing 16 different cache lines simultaneously, this is not coalesced. But since these are L2 reads, and multiple threads might hit the same SM's L1 or the shared L2, it might still be fine. But this is a problem because each thread generates a separate L2 request. Similarly for scalar_weight[64][80], for fixed i, threads access rows 0..15 at column i. Each row is 80 floats = 320 bytes. So threads access 0, 320, 640, etc., which are 320 bytes apart. 320/128 = 2.5 cache lines apart. Still not coalesced. This means weight reads from global memory will have poor coalescing. But since weights are tiny and repeated across all blocks, they'll be in L2 and partly in L1. Each SM's L1 can cache a few lines. With 16 threads in a warp accessing 16 different cache lines, the L1 might not be able to satisfy all from local cache; some might need L2 access. However, since the same weights are read by many warps/blocks, they should be well cached in L2. So the actual latency might still be low. But poor coalescing means more memory transactions. For example, with 16 threads accessing 16 different cache lines, we have 16 memory transactions per access pattern instead of 1-2. Can we transpose the weights to fix this? If we store scalar_weight as [80][64] instead of [64][80], then for fixed j, W_T[i][j] has threads accessing W_T[0][j], W_T[1][j], etc. But wait, in our case, each thread needs fixed j and varying i. If W is [64][80], then for fixed j, varying i, threads access different j values, not different i values. Hmm, let me re-examine. In the dot product: ```cuda for (i = 0; i < 80; i++) { sum += combined[i] * W[j][i]; } ``` For a fixed iteration i, threads in a warp have different j values (0..15, 16..31, etc. across the two groups). So for fixed i, threads access W[0][i], W[1][i], W[2][i], etc. These are in adjacent rows. If W is [64][80] row-major, W[0][i] and W[1][i] are 80 floats = 320 bytes apart. So for the scalar linear, within a warp with groups 0 and 1: - Group 0: threads 0-15 access W[0..15][i] - Group 1: threads 16-31 access W[0..15][i] (same rows!) Wait, both groups access the same scalar_weight rows 0..15? No, in group 0, each thread accesses one output. Thread 0 accesses W[0][i] for all i, thread 1 accesses W[1][i], etc. But each thread in group 0 computes 4 outputs. Thread 0 computes outputs 0, 16, 32, 48, so it accesses W[0][i], W[16][i], W[32][i], W[48][i]. For fixed i, within group 0 (threads 0-15): - Thread 0 accesses W[0][i] for its first output - Thread 1 accesses W[1][i] - ... - Thread 15 accesses W[15][i] So for iteration i of the first output, threads 0-15 access rows 0-15. These are 320 bytes apart. Not coalesced. But then for the second output, thread 0 accesses W[16][i], thread 1 accesses W[17][i], etc. The memory access pattern across iterations: thread t sequentially accesses W[t][0], W[t][1], ..., W[t][79] for its first output. This is contiguous! And for its second output: W[t+16][0..79] - also contiguous. So each thread individually has great memory access (contiguous within a row), but threads in a warp don't coalesce their accesses together. In other words, each thread generates contiguous accesses but different threads access different rows. On modern GPUs, this is handled by the memory system. Each warp's memory requests are merged per-cache-line. When threads in a warp access scattered cache lines, each cache line is fetched once. For 16 threads accessing 16 scattered cache lines, we get 16 cache line fetches. Since each cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats, and each row has 80 floats, a row spans 2.5 cache lines. So 16 rows span 40 cache lines. For each iteration i (single float), we'd need 16 cache lines. But with contiguous access within each thread, adjacent iterations hit the same cache line. So for the first output of each thread, thread t accesses W[t][0..79]. The first access to W[t][0] fetches the cache line containing W[t][0..31]. Then accesses W[t][1..31] are L1 cache hits. Then W[t][32] fetches the next cache line, etc. So each thread gets 3 cache line fetches for its 80 contiguous floats. Total cache line fetches per thread: 3 per output * 4 outputs = 12. For 16 threads: 192 cache line fetches total for scalar weights per item. If we had better coalescing, the 16 threads might have shared cache lines. But since W[t][0..31] and W[t+1][0..31] are on different cache lines, there's no sharing. For L2 cache, since all blocks access the same weights, these cache lines are likely in L2. So each thread fetches from L2 to L1. 192 L2 requests per item. For 1024 items: 196,608 L2 requests. The L2 bandwidth is much higher than HBM, so this should be fine. In fact, the weights are so small and repeatedly accessed that they might stay in L1 cache too, especially since each block processes 8 items and reuses the same weights. For 8 items per block, each block does 8 * 192 = 1536 cache line reads from scalar weights. But the weights are only 64 rows * 80 floats * 4 bytes = 20,480 bytes = 160 cache lines. So the block touches all 160 cache lines about 10 times. Once a cache line is in L1, subsequent accesses are L1 hits. So after the first few iterations, most weight accesses are L1 hits! The initial cold-start misses are insignificant. This means the scalar weight access pattern is actually fine. Similarly for gate_weight (16 rows of 80 floats): 16 * 2.5 = 40 cache lines. Very small. Vector_weight (16x16 = 256 floats = 8 cache lines): tiny. So I think keeping weights in global memory is fine. Now, another idea: instead of 16 threads per item, what if we use 32 threads per item but structure the work differently? With 32 threads per item: - Scalar output: 64 outputs / 32 threads = 2 per thread. Each thread does 2 dot products of 80. - Gate: 16 outputs / 32 threads = 0.5 per thread. Not clean. - Vector MM: 48 outputs / 32 threads = 1.5 per thread. Not clean. What if we assign threads to specific dimensions? 32 threads: - 16 threads for gate and vector (each thread does 1 gate + 1 vector row = 3 elements) - The other 16 threads help with scalar? Not easy to balance. What about 64 threads per item? - 64 scalar outputs / 64 = 1 per thread - 16 gate / 64 = only 16 active - 48 vector / 64 = only 48 active Too much idle time. I think 16 threads per item is the sweet spot. Actually, one more thought: could we have a thread do multiple items sequentially within a single block? Like, 128 threads per block, 16 threads per item, so 8 concurrent items. But each thread could process 2 or more items one after another. For example, 128 threads per block, processing 16 items total (8 concurrently, each thread handles 2 items serially). However, this doubles the work per thread while keeping the same number of blocks. Since we already have 1024 items / 8 = 128 blocks, and a block can only use one SM at a time, reducing blocks to 64 might underutilize the GPU. So 128 blocks with 128 threads each = good occupancy. No need for each thread to do multiple items. Let me now think about warp divergence. With 16 threads per item, and warp size 32, we have 2 items per warp. Threads 0-15 do item A, threads 16-31 do item B. Both groups execute the same instructions on the same clock, just different data. There should be no warp divergence since all 32 threads are active and taking the same code path. Wait, but what about the gate computation? Thread lane in 0..15 computes gate for its lane. All 16 threads in group 0 are active. And all 16 threads in group 1 are active. So the warp has all 32 threads active all the time. Great! The only difference is that threads 0-15 and 16-31 operate on different memory. So no divergence. Let me now write a complete kernel. Actually, I want to double check something. Here is my planned kernel code: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define TILE_M 16 #define ITEMS_PER_BLOCK 8 __global__ void se3_layer_kernel( const float* __restrict__ scalar_feat, const float* __restrict__ vector_feat, const float* __restrict__ scalar_weight, const float* __restrict__ scalar_bias, const float* __restrict__ vector_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_bias, float* __restrict__ scalar_out, float* __restrict__ vector_out, int B, int N, int scalar_in, int scalar_out_dim, int vector_in, int vector_out_dim ) { const int total_items = B * N; const int threads_per_item = TILE_M; // 16 const int items_per_block = blockDim.x / threads_per_item; int item_idx = blockIdx.x * items_per_block + threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int lane = threadIdx.x % threads_per_item; if (item_idx >= total_items) return; int b = item_idx / N; int n = item_idx % N; // Input/Output pointers const float* s_in = scalar_feat + item_idx * scalar_in; const float* v_in = vector_feat + item_idx * vector_in * 3; float* s_out_ptr = scalar_out + item_idx * scalar_out_dim; float* v_out_ptr = vector_out + item_idx * vector_out_dim * 3; // Shared memory for combined inputs and vector features // Layout: [items_per_block][scalar_in + vector_in] for combined // [items_per_block][vector_in * 3] for vectors extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* combined_shared = shared_mem; // size: items_per_block * (scalar_in + vector_in) float* v_shared = shared_mem + items_per_block * (scalar_in + vector_in); // size: items_per_block * vector_in * 3 int item_local = threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int combined_offset = item_local * (scalar_in + vector_in); int v_offset = item_local * vector_in * 3; // Load scalar inputs cooperatively // scalar_in = 64, threads_per_item = 16, so each thread loads 4 #pragma unroll for (int i = lane; i < scalar_in; i += threads_per_item) { combined_shared[combined_offset + i] = s_in[i]; } // Load vector inputs cooperatively // vector_in * 3 = 48, threads_per_item = 16, so each thread loads 3 #pragma unroll for (int i = lane; i < vector_in * 3; i += threads_per_item) { v_shared[v_offset + i] = v_in[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute vector norms and store in combined_shared[scalar_in + lane] float v0 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 0]; float v1 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 1]; float v2 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 2]; float norm = sqrtf(v0 * v0 + v1 * v1 + v2 * v2); combined_shared[combined_offset + scalar_in + lane] = norm; __syncthreads(); // Scalar linear: thread lane computes outputs lane, lane+16, lane+32, lane+48 #pragma unroll for (int out_idx = 0; out_idx < (scalar_out_dim / threads_per_item); out_idx++) { int j = lane + out_idx * threads_per_item; } // Gate linear: thread lane computes output lane float gate_sum = gate_bias[lane]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < scalar_in + vector_in; i++) { gate_sum += combined_shared[combined_offset + i] * gate_weight[lane * (scalar_in + vector_in) + i]; } float gate_val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate_sum)); // Vector transform: thread lane computes row lane (3 outputs) #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 3; j++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < vector_in; k++) { sum += v_shared[v_offset + k * 3 + j] * vector_weight[lane * vector_in + k]; } v_out_ptr[lane * 3 + j] = gate_val * sum; } } ``` Wait, there are a few issues: 1. `extern __shared__ float shared_mem[];` requires dynamic shared memory allocation. If 16 threads each load a scalar float at addresses `lane * 1` (in units of floats), then thread 0 reads address 0, thread 1 reads address 4 bytes, thread 2 reads address 8 bytes, etc. This is contiguous and perfectly coalesced into a single memory transaction. For a warp on Ampere/Hopper, a warp can load 128 bytes per transaction. So 16 scalar loads of 4 bytes each = 64 bytes, which fits in one transaction. Also fits in one 128-byte transaction. Wait, but these are for different items! Threads 0-15 and 16-31 are accessing different items' scalar features. If item A is at base address X, and item B is at base address Y, then threads 0-15 access X+0 to X+60, and threads 16-31 access Y+0 to Y+60. The memory controller merges requests from the same warp. So it may issue two transactions: one for X to X+127, and one for Y to Y+127. This is fine. The loads are still coalesced. So scalar loads are fine. No need for float4 unless we want to reduce instruction count slightly. Let me proceed with scalar loads for simplicity. Now, let me write the full Python + CUDA code. Actually, one more optimization idea. Instead of using shared memory for combined inputs, what if we use register shuffling? With shuffle, each thread keeps its own 4 scalar inputs and 1 norm in registers. For dot products, it gets other threads' values via shuffle. Let me count instructions: For scalar dot product of length 80: - 80 iterations - Each iteration: `__shfl_sync` + FMA - `__shfl_sync` is one instruction on modern GPUs For 4 dot products: 320 shuffles + 320 FMAs. For gate: 80 shuffles + 80 FMAs. For vector MM: - For each of 3 output elements: 16 shuffles + 16 FMAs - Total: 48 shuffles + 48 FMAs. Total per thread: 448 shuffles + 448 FMAs. On Hopper, FMA throughput is very high (can execute multiple per cycle per SM), and shuffle throughput is also high. The total instruction count is quite low. Compare with shared memory approach: For scalar dot product: - 80 shared loads + 80 FMAs per output - For 4 outputs: 320 loads + 320 FMAs - For gate: 80 loads + 80 FMAs - For vector: 48 loads + 48 FMAs Total: 448 loads + 448 FMAs Shared loads have latency ~20 cycles, while shuffle has latency ~1 cycle. But since FMAs and loads can be interleaved, and with loop unrolling, the effective throughput might be similar. However, shared memory has a potential advantage: all threads read the same shared memory location in each iteration (broadcast), which is very fast. But on Hopper, shared memory broadcast might also take a few cycles. Actually, on Ampere/Hopper, `__shfl_sync` is so fast that the shuffle approach might be better. It also avoids `__syncthreads()` calls and shared memory setup. Let me try the shuffle approach to avoid shared memory bank issues and synchronizations. Wait, I realize the shuffle approach has a problem with the "items per warp" setup. A warp has 32 threads. If we have 2 items per warp (16 threads each), then when threads 0-15 do `__shfl_sync(0xFFFF, val, k)`, they get values from lanes 0-15. But the mask 0xFFFF includes all 32 threads. The result for thread 0 is the value of `val` from lane `k` (which is in group 0). This is fine because lane k has the right item's data. Similarly for group 1 (threads 16-31), `__shfl_sync(0xFFFF, val, k)` gets from lane k, which is in group 0, not group 1! Oh no! This is a critical bug. `__shfl_sync` operates on lanes 0-31 of a warp. If threads 16-31 call `__shfl_sync(0xFFFF, val, 5)`, they get the value from lane 5, which belongs to item A, not item B. So for group 1, we need to add an offset. Thread 16 + lane in group 1 should call `__shfl_sync(0xFFFF, val, 16 + k)`. Since lane in group 0 is `threadIdx.x % 16`, and the warp lane is also `threadIdx.x % 32`, group 0 occupies lanes 0-15 and group 1 occupies lanes 16-31. So for group 0: `src_lane = k` (where k in 0..15) For group 1: `src_lane = 16 + k` But all 32 threads in the warp execute the same instruction. So `src_lane` must be warp-lane, meaning for group 0 it's `k`, for group 1 it's `16 + k`. In the code, we can compute: ```cuda int warp_lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int group_base = (warp_lane / 16) * 16; // Then src_lane = group_base + k ``` Since `group_base` is 0 for group 0 and 16 for group 1, and all threads in the warp compute it the same way? No, threads 0-15 get group_base=0, threads 16-31 get group_base=16. But this creates divergence only in the `src_lane` parameter, not in control flow. The `__shfl_sync` instruction takes a runtime `srcLane` parameter, so all threads in the warp execute the same instruction with the same `srcLane`. But since `group_base` is different for the two halves, this would be a problem if they tried to execute different instructions. However, `__shfl_sync` is a single instruction; each thread uses its own `srcLane` value (because `srcLane` is a register value). Wait, is `srcLane` in `__shfl_sync` a compile-time constant or runtime variable? `__shfl_sync(unsigned mask, T var, int srcLane, int width=warpSize)` On modern NVIDIA GPUs, `srcLane` can be a runtime variable. Each thread in the warp can use a different `srcLane` value. The hardware supports this. But there's a catch: if different threads in the warp have different `srcLane` values, the execution is still uniform (no divergence), but the hardware might need to do more work. Actually, for `srcLane` as a runtime variable, the warp still executes one `SHFL` instruction, but with per-thread `srcLane` registers. This is supported. So the shuffle approach can work with the correct lane calculation. Let me write the shuffle-based kernel: ```cuda __global__ void se3_layer_kernel(...) { // ... index setup ... int warp_lane = threadIdx.x % 32; // 0..31 within warp int group_base = (warp_lane / 16) * 16; int lane_in_group = warp_lane % 16; // Load scalar inputs: each thread loads 4 float s_local[4]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 4; i++) { s_local[i] = s_in[lane_in_group * 4 + i]; } // Load vector inputs: each thread loads 3 float v_local[3]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 3; i++) { v_local[i] = v_in[lane_in_group * 3 + i]; } // Compute norm float norm = sqrtf(v_local[0]*v_local[0] + v_local[1]*v_local[1] + v_local[2]*v_local[2]); // Compute scalar linear outputs #pragma unroll for (int out_idx = 0; out_idx < 4; out_idx++) { int j = lane_in_group + out_idx * 16; float sum = scalar_bias[j]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { int src_thread = group_base + i / 4; int src_reg = i % 4; float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s_local[src_reg], src_thread); sum += val * scalar_weight[j * 80 + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int src_thread = group_base + i; float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, norm, src_thread); sum += val * scalar_weight[j * 80 + 64 + i]; } s_out_ptr[j] = sum; } // Gate float gate_sum = gate_bias[lane_in_group]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 64; i++) { int src_thread = group_base + i / 4; int src_reg = i % 4; float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s_local[src_reg], src_thread); gate_sum += val * gate_weight[lane_in_group * 80 + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) { int src_thread = group_base + i; float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, norm, src_thread); gate_sum += val * gate_weight[lane_in_group * 80 + 64 + i]; } float gate_val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate_sum)); // Vector transform #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 3; j++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 16; k++) { int src_thread = group_base + k; float val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v_local[j], src_thread); sum += val * vector_weight[lane_in_group * 16 + k]; } v_out_ptr[lane_in_group * 3 + j] = gate_val * sum; } } ``` This should work correctly. But there are a lot of `__shfl_sync` calls. Let me think if this is actually efficient. The key question is: can the compiler optimize this? With `#pragma unroll`, yes, the loops get unrolled. Each iteration becomes a shuffle + FMA. The instruction scheduler can overlap FMAs with shuffles. However, 400 shuffle instructions per thread is a lot. Pipe utilization for shuffle is high but not infinite. Let me check if there's a better way. Actually, I realize there's a simpler approach: use a warp-level matrix multiplication or use shared memory more efficiently. What if we assign one thread to one (b,n) and just compute everything serially? Then we have 1024 threads, each with ~14K FLOPs. On H100, each SM can handle 2048 threads. With 1024 threads total and 132 SMs, we have ~8 threads per SM. But each thread does 14K FLOPs. At ~100 GFLOPS per thread? No, that's not how it works. Let me write the code now. ### Full Python + CUDA Code ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define TILE_M 16 template <int SI, int SO, int VI, int VO> __global__ void se3_layer_kernel( const float* __restrict__ scalar_feat, const float* __restrict__ vector_feat, const float* __restrict__ scalar_weight, const float* __restrict__ scalar_bias, const float* __restrict__ vector_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_bias, float* __restrict__ scalar_out, float* __restrict__ vector_out, int B, int N ) { const int total_items = B * N; const int threads_per_item = TILE_M; const int items_per_block = blockDim.x / threads_per_item; int item_idx = blockIdx.x * items_per_block + threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int lane = threadIdx.x % threads_per_item; if (item_idx >= total_items) return; const float* s_in = scalar_feat + item_idx * SI; const float* v_in = vector_feat + item_idx * VI * 3; float* s_out_ptr = scalar_out + item_idx * SO; float* v_out_ptr = vector_out + item_idx * VO * 3; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* combined_shared = shared_mem; float* v_shared = shared_mem + items_per_block * (SI + VI); int item_local = threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int combined_offset = item_local * (SI + VI); int v_offset = item_local * VI * 3; // Load scalar inputs #pragma unroll for (int i = lane; i < SI; i += threads_per_item) { combined_shared[combined_offset + i] = s_in[i]; } // Load vector inputs #pragma unroll for (int i = lane; i < VI * 3; i += threads_per_item) { v_shared[v_offset + i] = v_in[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute norm float v0 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 0]; float v1 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 1]; float v2 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 2]; float norm = sqrtf(v0 * v0 + v1 * v1 + v2 * v2); combined_shared[combined_offset + SI + lane] = norm; __syncthreads(); // Scalar linear: outputs lane, lane+16, lane+32, lane+48 #pragma unroll for (int out_idx = 0; out_idx < SO / threads_per_item; out_idx++) { int j = lane + out_idx * threads_per_item; float sum = scalar_bias[j]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < SI + VI; i++) { sum += combined_shared[combined_offset + i] * scalar_weight[j * (SI + VI) + i]; } s_out_ptr[j] = sum; } // Gate linear float gate_sum = gate_bias[lane]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < SI + VI; i++) { gate_sum += combined_shared[combined_offset + i] * gate_weight[lane * (SI + VI) + i]; } float gate_val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate_sum)); // Vector transform #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < 3; j++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < VI; k++) { sum += v_shared[v_offset + k * 3 + j] * vector_weight[lane * VI + k]; } v_out_ptr[lane * 3 + j] = gate_val * sum; } } std::vector<torch::Tensor> se3_layer_forward( torch::Tensor scalar_feat, torch::Tensor vector_feat, torch::Tensor scalar_weight, torch::Tensor scalar_bias, torch::Tensor vector_weight, torch::Tensor gate_weight, torch::Tensor gate_bias ) { int B = scalar_feat.size(0); int N = scalar_feat.size(1); int SI = scalar_feat.size(2); int VI = vector_feat.size(2); int SO = scalar_weight.size(0); int VO = vector_weight.size(0); auto scalar_out = torch::empty({B, N, SO}, scalar_feat.options()); auto vector_out = torch::empty({B, N, VO, 3}, vector_feat.options()); int total_items = B * N; const int threads_per_item = TILE_M; int items_per_block = 128 / threads_per_item; int num_blocks = (total_items + items_per_block - 1) / items_per_block; int shared_mem_size = items_per_block * ((SI + VI) + VI * 3) * sizeof(float); if (SI == 64 && SO == 64 && VI == 16 && VO == 16) { se3_layer_kernel<64, 64, 16, 16><<<num_blocks, 128, shared_mem_size>>>( scalar_feat.data_ptr<float>(), vector_feat.data_ptr<float>(), scalar_weight.data_ptr<float>(), scalar_bias.data_ptr<float>(), vector_weight.data_ptr<float>(), gate_weight.data_ptr<float>(), gate_bias.data_ptr<float>(), scalar_out.data_ptr<float>(), vector_out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N ); } else { // Fallback to generic version is not implemented in this kernel // For other sizes, we still need to handle them // Let me add a non-templated version or another template instantiation } return {scalar_out, vector_out}; } """ # ... ``` Wait, the issue is that `load_inline` function binding calls our C++ function from Python. Even without compile-time loop bounds, a well-written kernel should still be much faster than the reference due to fusion. Actually, wait. The `#pragma unroll` requires compile-time loop bounds. Without them, the compiler won't unroll, and there will be loop overhead. For loops of size 80, 16, 64, etc., loop overhead is negligible. The main performance gains come from fusing the operations, not from loop unrolling. Let me write a non-templated kernel. I'll keep the `#pragma unroll` where the bounds are compile-time constant (like `SO / threads_per_item` where `SO = 64` and `threads_per_item = 16`, giving 4, which is a compile-time constant if we write `4` explicitly). Hmm, but in a non-templated kernel, `SO` and `threads_per_item` are not compile-time constants. However, `threads_per_item` is `#define TILE_M 16`, so it is a compile-time constant. Let me now write the complete code. One more optimization: `__ldg()` for read-only global memory. This is a hint that the memory is read-only and can be cached in the read-only data cache. We can use it for weights and inputs. ```cuda float w = __ldg(&scalar_weight[idx]); ``` But `__ldg` only works for global memory. Using it for weights and inputs might help. Actually, on modern GPUs (Volta and later), there's no separate read-only cache; `__ldg` just uses the normal L1/L2 cache with a different policy. But it can still help with compiler optimizations. I'll keep it simple and use regular loads. Another thought: the `sqrtf` function. We can use `std::sqrt` or `sqrtf`. On CUDA, `sqrtf` is the fast single-precision sqrt. We should use `sqrtf` for fp32. Actually, for numerical accuracy, using `sqrtf` should match PyTorch's behavior. PyTorch uses `std::sqrt` in C++ which for float calls `sqrtf`. So it should match. For the sigmoid, PyTorch uses the standard formula `1 / (1 + exp(-x))`. But we need to be careful about numerical stability for large negative or positive x. PyTorch's sigmoid implementation uses: ```cpp float z = expf(-fabsf(x)); return (x >= 0) ? 1 / (1 + z) : z / (1 + z); ``` Or something similar to avoid overflow in `exp`. For our purposes, standard sigmoid should be fine since inputs are typically not extreme. Actually, let me check if PyTorch's implementation matches exactly. The tolerance is `atol=rtol=1e-4` for fp32. Even if our sigmoid has slightly different numerical behavior, it should still be within 1e-4. But to be safe, I'll use the numerically stable version: ```cuda float sigmoid(float x) { return (x >= 0.0f) ? 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)) : expf(x) / (1.0f + expf(x)); } ``` Wait, if x is very negative, `expf(x)` is small, `expf(x) / (1 + expf(x))` ≈ `expf(x)`. If x is very positive, `expf(-x)` is small, `1 / (1 + expf(-x))` ≈ 1. Both avoid overflow. For x = 0: first formula gives 1/(1+1) = 0.5. Second gives 1/(1+1) = 0.5. Let me unify: ```cuda float sigmoid(float x) { if (x >= 0.0f) { float z = expf(-x); return 1.0f / (1.0f + z); } else { float z = expf(x); return z / (1.0f + z); } } ``` This is numerically stable. But it introduces a branch. On a warp with mixed signs, there would be divergence. However, for typical neural network inputs, signs are usually mixed. PyTorch's sigmoid might use a custom formula that's branchless. Let me check if I can use `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))` and still be within tolerance. I'll just use the standard formula and see. The inputs to sigmoid come from a linear layer on normal-distributed data, so they'll be moderate. `expf(10)` is fine. `expf(-100)` is 0. So for typical values it should work. Actually, let me use the safer version to be sure. One more thing: should we use `Tanh` instead? No, reference uses sigmoid. OK, let me finalize the code.
