Here's my first draft of the complete code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define TILE_M 16 __global__ void se3_layer_kernel( const float* __restrict__ scalar_feat, const float* __restrict__ vector_feat, const float* __restrict__ scalar_weight, const float* __restrict__ scalar_bias, const float* __restrict__ vector_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_bias, float* __restrict__ scalar_out, float* __restrict__ vector_out, int B, int N, int SI, int SO, int VI, int VO ) { const int total_items = B * N; const int threads_per_item = TILE_M; const int items_per_block = blockDim.x / threads_per_item; int item_idx = blockIdx.x * items_per_block + threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int lane = threadIdx.x % threads_per_item; if (item_idx >= total_items) return; const float* s_in = scalar_feat + item_idx * SI; const float* v_in = vector_feat + item_idx * VI * 3; float* s_out_ptr = scalar_out + item_idx * SO; float* v_out_ptr = vector_out + item_idx * VO * 3; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* combined_shared = shared_mem; float* v_shared = shared_mem + items_per_block * (SI + VI); int item_local = threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int combined_offset = item_local * (SI + VI); int v_offset = item_local * VI * 3; // Load scalar inputs cooperatively for (int i = lane; i < SI; i += threads_per_item) { combined_shared[combined_offset + i] = s_in[i]; } // Load vector inputs cooperatively for (int i = lane; i < VI * 3; i += threads_per_item) { v_shared[v_offset + i] = v_in[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute vector norm for this thread's vector float v0 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 0]; float v1 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 1]; float v2 = v_shared[v_offset + lane * 3 + 2]; float norm = sqrtf(v0 * v0 + v1 * v1 + v2 * v2); combined_shared[combined_offset + SI + lane] = norm; __syncthreads(); // Scalar linear: each thread handles 4 outputs int num_scalar_outs_per_thread = SO / threads_per_item; for (int out_idx = 0; out_idx < num_scalar_outs_per_thread; out_idx++) { int j = lane + out_idx * threads_per_item; float sum = scalar_bias[j]; for (int i = 0; i < SI + VI; i++) { sum += combined_shared[combined_offset + i] * scalar_weight[j * (SI + VI) + i]; } s_out_ptr[j] = sum; } // Gate linear: each thread handles 1 output float gate_sum = gate_bias[lane]; for (int i = 0; i < SI + VI; i++) { gate_sum += combined_shared[combined_offset + i] * gate_weight[lane * (SI + VI) + i]; } float gate_val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate_sum)); // Vector transform: each thread handles 1 row (3 elements) for (int j = 0; j < 3; j++) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < VI; k++) { sum += v_shared[v_offset + k * 3 + j] * vector_weight[lane * VI + k]; } v_out_ptr[lane * 3 + j] = gate_val * sum; } } std::vector<at::Tensor> se3_layer_forward( at::Tensor scalar_feat, at::Tensor vector_feat, at::Tensor scalar_weight, at::Tensor scalar_bias, at::Tensor vector_weight, at::Tensor gate_weight, at::Tensor gate_bias ) { int B = scalar_feat.size(0); int N = scalar_feat.size(1); int SI = scalar_feat.size(2); int VI = vector_feat.size(2); int SO = scalar_weight.size(0); int VO = vector_weight.size(0); auto scalar_out = torch::empty({B, N, SO}, scalar_feat.options()); auto vector_out = torch::empty({B, N, VO, 3}, vector_feat.options()); int total_items = B * N; const int threads_per_item = TILE_M; int items_per_block = 128 / threads_per_item; int num_blocks = (total_items + items_per_block - 1) / items_per_block; int shared_mem_size = items_per_block * ((SI + VI) + VI * 3) * sizeof(float); se3_layer_kernel<<<num_blocks, 128, shared_mem_size>>>( scalar_feat.data_ptr<float>(), vector_feat.data_ptr<float>(), scalar_weight.data_ptr<float>(), scalar_bias.data_ptr<float>(), vector_weight.data_ptr<float>(), gate_weight.data_ptr<float>(), gate_bias.data_ptr<float>(), scalar_out.data_ptr<float>(), vector_out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, SI, SO, VI, VO ); return {scalar_out, vector_out}; } ''' # Compile se3_cuda = load_inline( name="se3_cuda", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["se3_layer_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, scalar_in, scalar_out, vector_in, vector_out): super().__init__() self.scalar_in = scalar_in self.scalar_out = scalar_out self.vector_in = vector_in self.vector_out = vector_out self.scalar_linear = nn.Linear(scalar_in + vector_in, scalar_out) self.vector_weight = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(vector_out, vector_in)) nn.init.xavier_uniform_(self.vector_weight) self.vector_gate = nn.Linear(scalar_in + vector_in, vector_out) def forward(self, scalar_feat, vector_feat): # Extract weights from PyTorch layers scalar_weight = self.scalar_linear.weight # (scalar_out, scalar_in + vector_in) scalar_bias = self.scalar_linear.bias # (scalar_out,) gate_weight = self.vector_gate.weight # (vector_out, scalar_in + vector_in) gate_bias = self.vector_gate.bias # (vector_out,) return se3_cuda.se3_layer_forward( scalar_feat, vector_feat, scalar_weight, scalar_bias, self.vector_weight, gate_weight, gate_bias ) ``` Wait, there's a CUDA runtime error potential. `load_inline` compiles at module load time. The kernel assumes contiguous float tensors. We should ensure the inputs are contiguous float tensors. Also, `.data_ptr<float>()` requires the tensor to be of float type. The inputs should be float32. One more issue: the `se3_layer_kernel` only works if: - `SO % TILE_M == 0` (each thread gets an equal number of scalar outputs) - `SI % TILE_M == 0` or the load loop works correctly - `VI == TILE_M` (exactly 16 vectors, each thread handles exactly one) For the generic case where VI might not equal 16, the kernel as written assumes lane < VI for norm computation, gate, and vector transform. If VO < 16, we'd have issues too. But for the test, VI=16 and VO=16, and SI=64, SO=64. So SO/TILE_M = 4. It works. To make it robust, for non-power-of-2 sizes, I should add bounds checking. But for the given problem, the sizes match perfectly. Actually, I just need to make sure that `vector_in * 3` is evenly divisible by `threads_per_item` for the cooperative load to work without gaps. `16 * 3 = 48`, and `48 / 16 = 3`. So each thread loads exactly 3 floats. Good. For scalar_in = 64, each thread loads `64 / 16 = 4` floats. Good. If sizes were different (e.g., scalar_in = 65), the load loop `for (int i = lane; i < SI; i += threads_per_item)` handles it correctly. But then `SI + VI` might not divide evenly for the dot products, and `SO / threads_per_item` might not be integer. For the generic case, I could add a bounds check in the kernel. However, for valid sizes where lane < VI and lane < VO, all threads stay active. PyTorch stores tensors in row-major order by default. A weight of shape (64, 80) is stored such that `weight[i, j]` is at `data_ptr[i * 80 + j]`. So `scalar_weight[j * (SI + VI) + i]` accesses column `i` of row `j`. This is correct for our dot product where output `j` needs all inputs `i`. These are all perfectly contiguous within each iteration, no bank conflicts. On modern GPUs, shared memory broadcast has no bank conflicts (or minimal). Great! For weights in global memory, we have scattered access as discussed, but weights are small and cached. reasoning
The issue seems to be that `load_inline` expects the function declaration in the cpp_sources, not the cuda_sources. Let me fix the code structure and provide a proper declaration. Also the scale is off: TILE_M=16 mapping for thread assignment on VI is wrong. VO and VI are both 16 so lane goes from 0 to 15, that's fine. But later I use `lane` for scalar out too, `SO=64`, need separate approach. Better approach: I'll just simplify, avoid the shared mem tile complexities and just use element-level parallelism. The kernel is memory bandwidth bound anyway. Let me write a cleaner kernel.

The problem is memory bandwidth bound - we have to read the same inputs multiple times. Let's think about fusing everything into a single kernel that can operate efficiently. For H100, I should think about: 1. Fusing the scalar and vector computations to avoid redundant norm computations and memory bandwidth 2. Using shared memory for weights to improve reuse 3. Better thread mapping - perhaps using block-level mapping where each block handles multiple (B,N) elements The dimensions: - B=8, N=128, so BN=1024 - scalar_in=64, scalar_out=64 - vector_in=16, vector_out=16 Total work: - scalar: 1024 * 64 = 65536 outputs - vector: 1024 * 16 * 3 = 49152 outputs For each (B,N) pair, we need to: 1. Read SI=64 scalar features 2. Read VI*3=48 vector features 3. Compute VI=16 norms 4. For each of SO=64 outputs: linear combination over 80 features (64+16) 5. For each of VO=16 outputs: compute gate from 80 features, compute 3 x linear combo of VI=16 vectors, multiply by gate The key insight is that we can do all this per (B,N) element and keep everything in registers/shared memory. Since BN=1024, and each element has relatively small dimension, we should map (B,N) to blocks and let threads within a block compute the outputs. With 128 threads, we could use 48 threads for vector and 64 for scalar, with some overlap. Actually, let me try a simpler approach: fusion of scalar and vector into a single kernel with each thread computing one output element, either scalar or vector. But the norms would be computed twice (once for scalar kernel, once for vector kernel). The current version does two kernels which each compute norms. Let me just make a more optimized version. Actually, wait - I realize the current simple version might be fine. But let me think about whether there are correctness issues with my current kernel. In the scalar kernel, I have: ```cuda float sum = scalar_bias[o]; ... scalar_out[idx] = sum; ``` In the vector kernel, I have: ```cuda float gate_val = gate_bias[o]; ... for (int j = 0; j < 3; ++j) { float vsum = 0.0f; ... vector_out[idx * 3 + j] = gate_val * vsum; } ``` Wait, in the original PyTorch model: ```python v_out = torch.einsum("oi,bnid->bnod", self.vector_weight, vector_feat) gate = torch.sigmoid(self.vector_gate(combined)).unsqueeze(-1) v_out = gate * v_out ``` So `v_out` shape is (B, N, VO, 3) and `gate` shape is (B, N, VO, 1). The gate is broadcast over the last dimension. In my kernel, `idx = bno * VO + o`, so `vector_out[idx * 3 + j]` corresponds to (b, n, o, j). And `gate_val` is the same for all j for a given (b,n,o). This is correct. The scalar part: ```python s_out = self.scalar_linear(combined) ``` This is just a linear layer. In my kernel, `scalar_out[idx]` where `idx = bns * SO + o` corresponds to (b, n, o). Correct. OK, the current implementation is correct. Now let me optimize it with a fused kernel. Let me write a cleaner, more optimized fused kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_se3_kernel( const float* scalar_feat, const float* vector_feat, const float* scalar_weight, const float* scalar_bias, const float* vector_weight, const float* gate_weight, const float* gate_bias, float* scalar_out, float* vector_out, int B, int N, int SI, int SO, int VI, int VO ) { const int items_per_block = 8; const int threads_per_item = blockDim.x / items_per_block; int item_local = threadIdx.x / threads_per_item; int tid = threadIdx.x % threads_per_item; int item_idx = blockIdx.x * items_per_block + item_local; if (item_idx >= B * N) return; extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float* combined = sdata + item_local * (SI + VI); float* vectors = sdata + items_per_block * (SI + VI) + item_local * (VI * 3); const float* s_in = scalar_feat + item_idx * SI; const float* v_in = vector_feat + item_idx * (VI * 3); // Load scalars for (int i = tid; i < SI; i += threads_per_item) { combined[i] = s_in[i]; } // Load vectors for (int i = tid; i < VI * 3; i += threads_per_item) { vectors[i] = v_in[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute norms for (int i = tid; i < VI; i += threads_per_item) { float v0 = vectors[i * 3 + 0]; float v1 = vectors[i * 3 + 1]; float v2 = vectors[i * 3 + 2]; combined[SI + i] = sqrtf(v0*v0 + v1*v1 + v2*v2); } __syncthreads(); float* s_out_ptr = scalar_out + item_idx * SO; float* v_out_ptr = vector_out + item_idx * (VO * 3); // Scalar linear outputs for (int o = tid; o < SO; o += threads_per_item) { float sum = scalar_bias[o]; for (int i = 0; i < SI + VI; ++i) { sum += combined[i] * scalar_weight[o * (SI + VI) + i]; } s_out_ptr[o] = sum; } // Vector outputs: gate + linear for (int o = tid; o < VO; o += threads_per_item) { float gate = gate_bias[o]; for (int i = 0; i < SI + VI; ++i) { gate += combined[i] * gate_weight[o * (SI + VI) + i]; } gate = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate)); for (int j = 0; j < 3; ++j) { float vsum = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < VI; ++i) { vsum += vectors[i * 3 + j] * vector_weight[o * VI + i]; } v_out_ptr[o * 3 + j] = gate * vsum; } } } ``` Wait, I need to double check: `item_idx * SI` for scalar_feat - scalar_feat shape is (B, N, SI), and its stride should indeed be N*SI or just SI depending on whether it's contiguous. Since I've called `.contiguous()`, the memory layout is (B*N*SI) with scalar_feat[b, n, i] at index (b*N + n)*SI + i. That's `item_idx * SI + i`. Yes, correct. For vector_feat: shape (B, N, VI, 3), vector_feat[b, n, i, j] at index ((b*N + n)*VI + i)*3 + j = item_idx * (VI*3) + i*3 + j. Yes, correct. For scalar_out: same as scalar_feat. Correct. For vector_out: same layout as vector_feat. Correct. For scalar_weight: shape (SO, SI+VI). Correct. For vector_weight: shape (VO, VI). Correct. For gate_weight: shape (VO, SI+VI). Correct. OK, now one more optimization: we could keep weights in shared memory too. Since weights are small: - scalar_weight: SO * (SI+VI) = 64 * 80 = 5120 floats = 20KB - vector_weight: VO * VI = 16 * 16 = 256 floats = 1KB - gate_weight: VO * (SI+VI) = 16 * 80 = 1280 floats = 5KB Total weights: ~26KB. With 8 items per block using the data arrays, plus weights: total shared mem per block would be about 8 * 80 * 4 + 8 * 48 * 4 + 26KB = ~2.5KB + 1.5KB + 26KB = ~30KB. That's well within the 227KB limit. Loading weights to shared memory once per block could help with memory access patterns. Actually, weights are read by all blocks. But with the small problem size, they might already be in L2 cache. Hmm, on H100 the L2 cache is 50MB or more, so all weights easily fit. So loading weights to shared memory isn't critical, but it might help with coalescing. Actually, looking at the weight access pattern: - In my current kernels, weights are loaded from global memory with good coalescing because adjacent threads access adjacent elements (o * (SI+VI) + i where o differs by 1). Actually, for scalar outputs, thread with idx computes output o. So threads with adjacent idx have adjacent o. When accessing weight[o * (SI+VI) + i], adjacent threads access adjacent weights, which is coalesced. This is good. One issue: in the loop `for (int i = 0; i < SI + VI; ++i)`, all threads read the same weight element in lockstep. This is broadcast, which is actually fast on modern GPUs because it hits L1 cache. So the current access pattern is already decent. Wait, but there's another issue: the vector computation has `for (int j = 0; j < 3; ++j)` as the outer loop and `for (int i = 0; i < VI; ++i)` as the inner loop. This means we access vector_weight[o * VI + i] for i=0..15 in the inner loop. Since o is constant for a given thread, and VI=16, we access 16 floats. But adjacent threads (different o) access different parts of vector_weight. This isn't coalesced. Actually, let me reconsider. For vector_weight, shape is (VO, VI) = (16, 16). Threads o=0 to o=15 access row 0 to row 15. For a fixed i, vector_weight[o * VI + i] is strided by VI=16. Adjacent threads access elements separated by 16 floats = 64 bytes. This isn't perfectly coalesced but it's OK. For vectors[i * 3 + j], with j varying in the inner loop (or i varying), this is coalesced since adjacent i values are adjacent in memory. Let me also think about whether I should use `__ldg` (cached loads) for the weight accesses. Since weights are read many times and are small, `__ldg` could help. Actually `__ldg` is for read-only data that can use the texture/L1 cache aggressively. Let me try using `const __restrict__` which should already enable many optimizations. One more thing: I should check if the problem is actually compute-bound or memory-bound. Let me compute arithmetic intensity. Fused kernel per (b,n): - Load: 64 + 48 = 112 floats = 448 bytes - Compute: ~10000 FLOPs - Store: 64 + 48 = 112 floats = 448 bytes - AI = 10000 / 896 = 11.2 FLOP/byte At 3.35 TB/s memory and 67 TFLOPS compute, the ridge point is 67,000 / 3.35 = 20 FLOP/byte. Our AI is 11.2, so we're actually memory-bound! That means fusing is very important to reduce memory bandwidth. With fused kernel, we read 112 floats and write 112 floats = 224 floats = 896 bytes. With two kernels, we read 112 floats twice and write separately = 336 floats read + 112 floats written = 448 floats = 1792 bytes. So fused kernel halves memory bandwidth. That's a big win. Let me also consider whether I can use vectorized loads/stores. For loading scalar and vector features, I could use float4 to load 4 floats at a time. Since SI=64 and VI*3=48, both are divisible by 4, so this works. Actually, a simpler optimization: use `float4` for vector loads where possible. Hmm, for now let me focus on getting the fused kernel right. Float4 optimization can be added later. Let me write and test the fused kernel. Actually, one more thought: in my current two-kernel design, I can see a potential issue. The vector kernel doesn't use `scalar_out`, so the compiler might optimize things differently. But it also means we launch two kernels from Python, which has some overhead (~10-20 microseconds per kernel launch). For a small problem that might take ~50 microseconds total, 2 kernel launches is significant overhead. A single fused kernel would eliminate this overhead. Let me code up the fused version and test it.

The fused kernel compiles and passes correctness. Now let me optimize further. The current kernel uses: - 128 threads per block, 16 items per block - Each item is a (B,N) point - Threads within the warp process different outputs for the same item This is actually not a great warp organization. Let me think about a better approach. For H100, we want to maximize occupancy and memory bandwidth. Let me try a few things: 1. Use a warp per item rather than 8 threads per item 2. Use vectorization for loads/stores 3. Consider using row-major weight matrices more effectively Actually, let me look at the performance. With 16 items per block and 128 threads, each warp (32 threads) covers 2 items with 16 threads each. This is already decent, but maybe I can use 8 warps per block = 4 items per block with 8 warps each = 256 threads. Wait, actually each item has: - SI + VI = 80 combined features - SO = 64 scalar outputs - VO = 16 vector outputs With 8 threads per item doing 8 outputs at a time, each thread processes only 8 outputs. For SO=64, each thread does 8 outputs, which is a nice number. But for VO=16, each thread does only 2 outputs with 8 threads. Still fine. Let me try tuning the threads per item and block configuration. Let me also try the approach of a warp per item (32 threads): - Items per block = 128 threads / 32 = 4 items - Each item gets 1 warp - For scalar (SO=64), each thread does 2 scalar outputs - For vector (VO=16), each thread does 1 vector output (with 3 components) Let me also try to register the weights, or use unrolled loops with known sizes. Better yet, let me hardcode the sizes in templates. Since the sizes are fixed (64, 64, 16, 16, 128, 8), I can use template specializations that the compiler can optimize aggressively. But `load_inline` with templates can be tricky. Let me try using constexpr if possible, or just rely on the compiler to unroll with `#pragma unroll`. Actually, I can use template parameters since they're fixed at compile time in our kernel: ```cpp template <int SI, int VI, int SO, int VO> __global__ void kernel(...) ``` And then dispatch based on runtime values using if-else or a switch. But since SI=64, VI=16, SO=64, VO=16, I can just hardcode them or use templates. Let me try a more optimized version with: 1. Warp per item (32 threads) 2. Each warp cooperatively processes all outputs for its item 3. Register caching for weights 4. Better use of shared memory for the combined features Actually, with a warp per item: - Combined array: 80 floats - Vector array: 48 floats - Per block (4 items): 320 + 192 = 512 floats = 2KB shared memory - Very small, we can fit many blocks Let me write this version. Wait, with only 4 items per block and B*N=1024 items, we'd need 256 blocks. With 128 SMs, that's 2 blocks per SM, which is not great occupancy. Let me go back to more items per block. With 16 items per block and 8 threads per item, we get: - 128 threads per block - 1024/16 = 64 blocks - 64 blocks / 128 SMs = 0.5 blocks per SM initially But as threads finish, more blocks can be scheduled. The limit is occupancy. Actually, the better metric is total threads. 64 blocks * 128 threads = 8192 threads total. H100 can run thousands of threads per SM. Let me try a different organization: one thread per item, and each thread loops over all outputs independently. This means: - 1024 threads total - Threads per block = 256 - Blocks = 4 - Very few blocks That's bad. We want at least hundreds of blocks to saturate the GPU. What about: - Each warp (32 threads) processes one item - So items_per_block = warps_per_block - Let warps_per_block = 8, so threads = 256, items = 8 - Blocks = 1024/8 = 128 This gives 128 blocks of 256 threads = 32768 threads total, which is good. Let me just try to optimize memory access patterns and instruction level parallelism. One issue: scalar_weight is accessed as `scalar_weight + o * (SI + VI) + i`. Each thread in a warp accesses the same row `o` but different `i`. This is coalesced if threads access adjacent elements. In my current code, threads within a warp have `o = tid`, and they process different `o` values, so they access different rows. This means it's NOT coalesced within a warp, because thread 0 accesses row 0, thread 1 accesses row 1, etc. The addresses are spaced by (SI+VI) floats. This is actually stride-80, which is bad for coalescing because the cache line is 32 floats and threads are accessing positions 0, 80, 160, ... which are 320 bytes apart. A 128-byte cache line holds only 1 of these accesses. Wait, the closest thread would access 80 floats apart. 80*4 bytes = 320 bytes. A cache line is typically 128 bytes on H100. I want to make sure the math is correct. ```cpp __global__ void fused_se3_kernel( const float* __restrict__ scalar_feat, const float* __restrict__ vector_feat, const float* __restrict__ scalar_weight, const float* __restrict__ scalar_bias, const float* __restrict__ vector_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_weight, const float* __restrict__ gate_bias, float* __restrict__ scalar_out, float* __restrict__ vector_out, int B, int N, int SI, int SO, int VI, int VO ) { const int item_idx = blockIdx.x; We need to reduce across the 16 active threads. We can use a mask approach or just do the full 32-thread reduction. Actually, the simpler approach: ```cpp // First, put the sum from thread i into thread i+16 float vsum0_16 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, vsum0, 16); vsum0 += (lane < 16) ? vsum0_16 : 0; // Then reduce the lower 16 threads for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { vsum0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffff, vsum0, offset); } // Now lane 0 has the full sum ``` Or even simpler, since threads 16-31 have vsum0=0, the full 32-thread reduction works correctly. import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_source = '''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <math.h>

__device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) {
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
        val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset);
    }
    return val;
}

__global__ void fused_se3_kernel_v2(
    const float* __restrict__ scalar_feat,
    const float* __restrict__ vector_feat,
    const float* __restrict__ scalar_weight,
    const float* __restrict__ scalar_bias,
    const float* __restrict__ vector_weight,
    const float* __restrict__ gate_weight,
    const float* __restrict__ gate_bias,
    float* __restrict__ scalar_out,
    float* __restrict__ vector_out,
    int B, int N, int SI, int SO, int VI, int VO
) {
    const int item_idx = blockIdx.x;
    if (item_idx >= B * N) return;
    
    const int lane = threadIdx.x % 32;
    const int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32;
    const int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32;
    
    extern __shared__ float smem[];
    float* combined = smem;
    float* vectors = smem + (SI + VI);
    float* gate_vals = vectors + (VI * 3);
    
    const float* s_in = scalar_feat + item_idx * SI;
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < SI; i += blockDim.x) {
        combined[i] = s_in[i];
    }
    
    const float* v_in = vector_feat + item_idx * (VI * 3);
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < VI * 3; i += blockDim.x) {
        vectors[i] = v_in[i];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < VI; i += blockDim.x) {
        float v0 = vectors[i * 3 + 0];
        float v1 = vectors[i * 3 + 1];
        float v2 = vectors[i * 3 + 2];
        combined[SI + i] = sqrtf(v0 * v0 + v1 * v1 + v2 * v2);
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    float* s_out_ptr = scalar_out + item_idx * SO;
    float* v_out_ptr = vector_out + item_idx * (VO * 3);
    
    const int scalar_iters = (SO + warps_per_block - 1) / warps_per_block;
    for (int iter = 0; iter < scalar_iters; iter++) {
        int o = iter * warps_per_block + warp_id;
        if (o >= SO) continue;
        
        float partial = 0.0f;
        const float* w_row = scalar_weight + o * (SI + VI);
        for (int i = lane; i < SI + VI; i += 32) {
            partial += combined[i] * w_row[i];
        }
        partial = warp_reduce_sum(partial);
        
        if (lane == 0) {
            s_out_ptr[o] = partial + scalar_bias[o];
        }
    }
    
    const int gate_iters = (VO + warps_per_block - 1) / warps_per_block;
    for (int iter = 0; iter < gate_iters; iter++) {
        int o = iter * warps_per_block + warp_id;
        if (o >= VO) continue;
        
        float partial = 0.0f;
        const float* gw_row = gate_weight + o * (SI + VI);
        for (int i = lane; i < SI + VI; i += 32) {
            partial += combined[i] * gw_row[i];
        }
        partial = warp_reduce_sum(partial);
        
        if (lane == 0) {
            gate_vals[o] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-(partial + gate_bias[o])));
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    for (int iter = 0; iter < gate_iters; iter++) {
        int o = iter * warps_per_block + warp_id;
        if (o >= VO) continue;
        
        float g = gate_vals[o];
        const float* vw_row = vector_weight + o * VI;
        
        float vsum0 = 0.0f, vsum1 = 0.0f, vsum2 = 0.0f;
        for (int i = lane; i < VI; i += 32) {
            float w = vw_row[i];
            vsum0 += vectors[i * 3 + 0] * w;
            vsum1 += vectors[i * 3 + 1] * w;
            vsum2 += vectors[i * 3 + 2] * w;
        }
        vsum0 = warp_reduce_sum(vsum0);
        vsum1 = warp_reduce_sum(vsum1);
        vsum2 = warp_reduce_sum(vsum2);
        
        if (lane == 0) {
            v_out_ptr[o * 3 + 0] = g * vsum0;
            v_out_ptr[o * 3 + 1] = g * vsum1;
            v_out_ptr[o * 3 + 2] = g * vsum2;
        }
    }
}

std::vector<torch::Tensor> se3_layer_forward(
    torch::Tensor scalar_feat,
    torch::Tensor vector_feat,
    torch::Tensor scalar_weight,
    torch::Tensor scalar_bias,
    torch::Tensor vector_weight,
    torch::Tensor gate_weight,
    torch::Tensor gate_bias
) {
    int B = scalar_feat.size(0);
    int N = scalar_feat.size(1);
    int SI = scalar_feat.size(2);
    int VI = vector_feat.size(2);
    int SO = scalar_weight.size(0);
    int VO = vector_weight.size(0);

    auto scalar_out = torch::empty({B, N, SO}, scalar_feat.options());
    auto vector_out = torch::empty({B, N, VO, 3}, vector_feat.options());

    int total_items = B * N;
    int threads = 256;
    int shared_size = ((SI + VI) + VI * 3 + VO) * sizeof(float);

    fused_se3_kernel_v2<<<total_items, threads, shared_size>>>(
        scalar_feat.data_ptr<float>(),
        vector_feat.data_ptr<float>(),
        scalar_weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        scalar_bias.data_ptr<float>(),
        vector_weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        gate_weight.data_ptr<float>(),
        gate_bias.data_ptr<float>(),
        scalar_out.data_ptr<float>(),
        vector_out.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, N, SI, SO, VI, VO
    );

    return {scalar_out, vector_out};
}
'''

cpp_header = '''
#include <torch/extension.h>
std::vector<torch::Tensor> se3_layer_forward(
    torch::Tensor scalar_feat,
    torch::Tensor vector_feat,
    torch::Tensor scalar_weight,
    torch::Tensor scalar_bias,
    torch::Tensor vector_weight,
    torch::Tensor gate_weight,
    torch::Tensor gate_bias
);
'''

se3_cuda = load_inline(
    name="se3_cuda_v2",
    cpp_sources=cpp_header,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["se3_layer_forward"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
    verbose=False,
)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, scalar_in, scalar_out, vector_in, vector_out):
        super().__init__()
        self.scalar_in = scalar_in
        self.scalar_out = scalar_out
        self.vector_in = vector_in
        self.vector_out = vector_out

        self.scalar_linear = nn.Linear(scalar_in + vector_in, scalar_out)
        self.vector_weight = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(vector_out, vector_in))
        nn.init.xavier_uniform_(self.vector_weight)
        self.vector_gate = nn.Linear(scalar_in + vector_in, vector_out)

    def forward(self, scalar_feat, vector_feat):
        scalar_weight = self.scalar_linear.weight
        scalar_bias = self.scalar_linear.bias
        gate_weight = self.vector_gate.weight
        gate_bias = self.vector_gate.bias

        return se3_cuda.se3_layer_forward(
            scalar_feat.contiguous(),
            vector_feat.contiguous(),
            scalar_weight,
            scalar_bias,
            self.vector_weight,
            gate_weight,
            gate_bias
        )
